### Post by jgrabham on 2007-04-19
Looking for a CD on the shelf and I found a manual for the first computer I ever used.  It was made in 1994 (I was born in 1992) It had DOS 6.22 and Windows 3.11 for workgroups.  4MB Ram (seems to have been upgraded as Ive found a reciept for 2X16mb modules) 250mb HDD and a 486DX intel proccessor. It had 1mb on board video.  I know my mum had a computer before it, but she cant remember it (im guessing a 286).  This computer was thrown out a couple of years ago. :(

Whats everyone elses first computer?

P.S. I have a C64 which I found in my Grandmas loft, but it doesnt work, turn it on and a bright purple screen appears instead of the blue  command thingy

### Post by askreet on 2007-04-19
The first computer I owned was a Tandy 286 when I was roughly 8 years old.  Some years later after it bit the dust I bought myself a used 386 that ran Window 3.1.  I remember programming a password in QBASIC using a third-party compiler and adding it to my autoexec.bat file to keep my sister off the PC!

Thanks for bringing back the memories!

### Post by matthew on 2007-04-19
> **yabbadabbadont said:**
> I had version 2.  Unlike the first model, it had a more normal keyboard instead of "calculator keys".  I don't like to remember how much I paid to expand it from 16 to 64KB of memory...  :)I remember upgrading the ram...it was expensive. I went from 4 to 32KB.

I remember the day we bought the disk drive to replace the cassette storage. I was so excited! 180KB on ONE disk!!! Wow! I may never need more than one or two disks. :) By the time I moved on, in 1989, I had hundreds.

Aahhh. Great memories.

### Post by mdsmedia on 2007-04-19
Mine (other than a handheld computer that you could program in very basic basic) was an XT with single 5.25" floppy drive, 20MB HDD, with DOS (I seem to remember it was PC DOS rather than MS DOS...but that changed pretty soon after I think) v4.x.....and 64K RAM.

I remember the joy of being able to change the text color from green to amber...maybe I couldn't on that particular PC.

PCWrite was my first word processor on that PC. I later got an EGA monitor (the joys of Kings Quest etc. :))

### Post by skwishybug on 2007-04-19
First computer was a Texas Instrument TI99/4A as well, but without the floppy drive. Did have a tape recording system where programs were saved as audio files. And the collection of games that were unique to the TI: Munchman, TI Invaders, Parsec.

Second computer was a Commodore PC20-III (8088 ) with 640K of RAM. Learned a lot about DOS with that machine.

### Post by AusIV4 on 2007-04-19
My first computer was an Apple II. It was a hand me down when my dad upgraded. I believe I was two or three. My dad has a commodore 64 in his basement, which he uses as a stand to hold up his modem and router - he can't bring himself to get rid of it.

When I was 17, my dad and stepmother had a daughter (my only sibling), and I built my sister a computer from partsI had lying around for her second birthday. By two and a half she's quite a wiz at her games. She can turn on her computer, click an icon, then go and complain to our dad when the CD for that game isn't in the tray and she can't reach the CD drive to put it in (not that she could figure out which CD she needed or put it in right side up if she could reach the tray). Right now it's running WIndows 98 (no internet connection, so no concern about viruses), but this summer I may see how many of her games will run on Wine, and consider putting Edubuntu on the computer for her.

### Post by Zuuswa on 2007-04-20
I feel so young . . . I dont remember the exact year, or specs of the rig, but it was a packard bell with less than 100 mhz pentium.  I played Descent on the thing till my eyes bled.  When we first got it, it had windows 3.1 on it, and I hated it and never used it.  Then my mom upgraded it to wondows 95 because the company she worked for finally got her a computer in her office and it had 95 on it.  I remember she had me teach her how to program in visual basic, and let me use the companies liscence to install it on the home 'puter.  Before that, I had learned Basic on an old V-tech toy.  I was probably 4-5 years old when I started on that thing . . . good times.

### Post by jariku on 2007-04-20
My first computer was a [Salora Manager]("http://www.old-computers.com/MUSEUM/computer.asp?c=1118") I got for Christmas in 1986 or 1987. My second computer was an [IBM PS/1 286]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_PS/1") I got in late 1990 or early 1991 when my brother switched to a 386. Around 1995 I got a Pentium computer and in 1997 an internet connection and it's all been downhill since!

The Salora Manager I had is still alive, but I don't have it anymore. It's mostly useless, since the external 5,25" floppy drive isn't working and it's suffered a few blows during the years (a pet ate it's power cord and a clumsy someone*COUGH*me*COUGH* dropped the computer once and it lost some keys). I don't know about the 286, though. It's 3,5" floppy drive broke and that rendered it mostly useless also. I gave it to my other brother, but I don't know what he did with it.

### Post by Chilli Bob on 2007-04-20
In 1982 I won a  [VZ200]("http://old-computers.com/museum/computer.asp?c=979") in a school competition.8k of memory and saving onto a cassete player. I loved it. Looking at the photo in the link I can still remember what it smelled like. Is that perverse?

My first PC was a Sperry brand XT clone with no hard drive and twin 360k floppies and green screen. Those were the days!  Actually I miss ascii word processors. I found them easier to look at for long periods than the modern day equivalent. Even when I got Win 3.1 (On a 386DX40 - the most powerfull computer of anyone I knew at the time.), I still used the shareware program PC-Write rather than the word processor in Works.

I'm showing my age.

### Post by weekend warrior on 2007-04-20

Ah the memories... guess that means I've been using computers over a quarter century now. It's been an interesting ride to say the least.

### Post by DrOlaf on 2007-04-20
The first computer I used was a Commodore PET. My school bought one right in the middle of my CSE Computer Studies course (now THERE is a useful qualification :) ). Before then we had to write out our BASIC code on sheets of paper, and one of the teachers would take them to the local Technical College, type them in, run them and give us the results printout. We had quite a long debug cycle :) .

The first computer I bought was a [Sinclair QL]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinclair_QL") when I was at college. Gotta love those tape-based microdrives.

Now, my favorite computer of the ones I have bought was one I picked up from my current University's surplus office supply/junk depot. I spotted a [NeXTstation]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NeXTstation"), with its laser printer, sitting in the corner. The guy there had no idea what it was and let me have them both for $50. When I consider what my professor paid for his when I was a graduate student....

---

### Post by mips on 2007-04-20
> **DrOlaf said:**

Yeah they were VERY pricey back in the day. I always salivated over them back in the day, and after that I was salivating over the SGI workstations. What model NeXT do you have ?

You can get the same desktop experience on Linux with AfterStep, GNUStep, OpenStep, WindowMaker for those that are nostalgic.

### Post by DrOlaf on 2007-04-20
> **mips said:**
> Yeah they were VERY pricey back in the day. I always salivated over them back in the day, and after that I was salivating over the SGI workstations. What model NeXT do you have ?

The guy in the depot had no idea why I was so excited when I saw it. He had no idea what it was - it had been discarded by the Math department a few weeks before, and was just gathering dust on a shelf. At least he didn't use my excitement as an excuse to put up the price. 

The model is a black-and-white NeXTStation (the "slab", not the "cube", for those that remember them). It has a 25MHz Motorola 68040,  a 400MB internal SCSI hard drive, and the 2.88MB floppy that never took off with other machines. It was DOA when I got it home (probably why it was discarded) but I got a replacement PSU from eBay and now it boots up fine. The display is what you would expect from an 18-year old CRT, but is readable. I even got my old prof to give me his system disks so I could do a fresh install. 

I used to love using these when I was a grad student. They were just so much cooler than the SparcStations that were available at the time (speaking of which, I have an SS10 lying around - I wonder if I should try putting Ubuntu on that?). 

> **mips said:**
> You can get the same desktop experience on Linux with AfterStep, GNUStep, OpenStep, WindowMaker for those that are nostalgic.

I tried that out myself on my Ubuntu desktop. It found it to be quite usable, and wicked fast.

In case anyone is wondering what the fuss is about, this is what they looked like:

### Post by forrestcupp on 2007-04-20
Commodore 64 - 64Kb RAM, ~2 mhz processor
started with a tape drive and amber monitor
upgraded to 1540 disk drive and a color tv
later I was lucky enough to have a 1570 dual-side disk drive and an external 1200 baud modem.  I used the modem to access various local BBS's and an online service for Commodores called Quantum Link.

I dreamed about being like the kids on the show The Whiz Kids.

I programmed and freelance marketed an adventure game called "Quest for Truth."  If anyone actually has a copy of this, please let me know.

### Post by Compyx on 2007-04-21
> **forrestcupp said:**
> Commodore 64 - 64Kb RAM, **~2 mhz processor**

You sure about 2 MHz? I think you're thinking about the C128, which could run at ~2MHz (by setting some bit in $d030), the C64's run at about 1MHz, 0.987Mhz for PAL systems and ~1.02MHz for NTSC systems.

I remember a guy telling me he might have found a way to make the CPU run at 2 or even 4 MHz: the next time I met him his C64 was fried, of course this had nothing to do with his little experiment, so he claimed :D

---

### Post by twowheeler on 2007-04-21
Heh, all you young whipper snappers ...

The first comp I ever used was the teletype machine at my high school that could dial in to the DEC PDP-11 at the school district building.  The modem was the kind that had rubber cups so that you could set the telephone handset on it after you dialed the number.  You could send characters at the blazing speed of 110 baud so if you typed fast enough you could overrun the buffer.  It just ran Basic.  If you wanted to save your program, you could print to the paper tape attachment, with holes punched for data recording.  Now that was cool.  :) 

That would have been 1975 or so.

The first one I owned though was a 286 with 1 MB of ram.  I paid extra to get the 10 mhz speed model.  It was from PC's Limited, the company which later became Dell.  A monochrome monitor in a kind of sick orange color.  Dang, it was expensive too, at $2,000.

Beat that!

:popcorn:

### Post by forrestcupp on 2007-04-21
> **Compyx said:**
> You sure about 2 MHz? I think you're thinking about the C128, which could run at ~2MHz (by setting some bit in $d030), the C64's run at about 1MHz, 0.987Mhz for PAL systems and ~1.02MHz for NTSC systems.

Yeah, that sounds right.  I remember 1.02 mhz on my NTSC system.

Also, for my C64 I ended up getting an Okimate color printer.  That was an amazing thing in the commodore days.  I think it had 3 colored heat transfer ribbons and it had to print each line 3 times.  Slow, but cool.  The things it printed looked better than what the C64 was able to display on the screen.

### Post by bonzodog on 2007-04-21
My first Computer was also, like some others here, a Sinclair ZX-81, followed by a ZX-Spectrum 16k, followed by the 48k, followed by the Spectrum +, which had a proper keyboard, and a built in tape drive!. We then got a Commodore 16, followed by a Commodore Plus 4 (weird piece of kit, blew up on us one day), followed by a Commodore 128.

Then, came the first PC, and old Toshiba 8086 with 2 5.25" floppy drives, a monochrome monitor (orange), and wordperfect on DOS 3.0. 

That one was owned by my grandparents, my grandmother was a secretary for the local council. 
The first PC I got my hands on was a true Unix PC, a Hewlett-Packard in 1989, with a touch sensitive monitor and HP-UX on it.  

I then had a break from owning PC's until 1997, when I got a Pentium 1 PC 133MHz, with 128MB of Ram, and windows 98se. Suffice to say, I used windows for about a week, tried to litestep it, (my experience with Linux and the net the previous year had opened my eyes, and found the windows UI unusable), accidentally removed shell.ini, and got a General Protection fault.

I had bought a Linux book the previous week, and it came with Caldera 1.3 (later taken over by SCO), so I installed that, and have never gone back. It has been Linux as a sole OS since then on all the home PC's.

### Post by roachk71 on 2007-04-21
My first was a Timex/Sinclair 1000, back in 1983...

Everybody would find me either watching Robotech, or *trying* to write a program for that computer (I was among those who absolutely _despised_ its membrane keyboard!) It was tedious, to say the least. And its display routines were very slow.

The very first computer I've used, though, was a 32k Apple II Plus in grade school. Come to think of it, in the early '90s I owned two Apples: a II Plus, and //e, and had 'em networked through their serial cards...Memories indeed.

My favorite hack on the //e was writing programs that used the 80-column card's (undocumented) double-hires feature!

### Post by yabbadabbadont on 2007-04-21
> **DougieFresh4U said:**
> Radio Shack Model 4 back in 1979 and at work I used an IBM 5252 (remember punch cards)?

My dad taught me how to read EBCDIC off of punch cards when I was in grade school.  He was attending a local community college on the GI Bill and they only had one keypunch machine that printed what was punched onto the top of the cards.  (and dad is a lousy typist...)  He'd give the cards to me as he punched them and I would write what was punched on the top of the card.  That way he could catch typo's while he was still at the machine.    I also remember the cardboard clock face that only had an hour hand on it.  It was beneath the sign that read, "The system will be down until..."  Ahhh, the good old days.  :lol:

### Post by fistfullofroses on 2007-04-22
The first computer I ever had was an 8088. It had 64kb ram, and a 2mb HD. 5 1/2 floppy. I eventually had a 14.4 kbps modem, and eventually had a 3 1/4 floppy drive but it was 720kb not 1.44mb. I used a braile display. I was running some form of unix at the time. I do not remember exactly what. I know in 93 I toyed with MINIX and then heard about Linux on a BB thus I switched, and shortly thereafter I built a new machine seeing as I was ceasing to be able to work well with others on my by then antiquated hardware. Seems such a far cry from what I am now running. That computer was around 2000 USD when I got it for my birth day. Now I can put together a machine for around a quarter of that price that usually has hundreds of times it's power.

### Post by Nimefurahi on 2007-04-22
My very first hobby "computer" was one that I had built in 1965. It had a singular purpose, that of solving quadratic equations via truth tables. I had fun making flip-flops and counters, "and" gates and "or" gates  all utilizing 6SN7 "valves" or vacuum tubes as they're called in the States. Ah yes, those were the days! Then came along transistors. Wow! My home built machines became much smaller in size.

But my very first "store bought" computer was the Scottish built Sinclair ZX-81. I quickly had to learn how to program assembly language for the Z80 processor where every byte had a purpose. (I sure wish today's young programmers could do the same )  Best thing I ever did with that ol' ZX-81 was to upgrade the RAM to 16kb and turn it into a spread-spectrum analyzer that could also control my 10 meter ham radio (homebrew).

I only share this as I've seen a few here on the thread who are near to me in age.

You'll excuse me now. I'm off for a nice bowl of stewed prunes.

---

### Post by pseudonym on 2007-04-22

In the 80s I wasn't big in to computers, but I used to play around sometimes on friends' Vic 20s and Apple IIs. But the first 'real' computer that I bought for myself was a 386 SX33 with 4MB RAM, an 80MB hard drive, some early 3D video card whose name I've long since forgotten, and an ISA Soundblaster. That was 1993 and I have fond memories of playing Wolfenstein 3D into the wee hours. I can't say I miss Windows 3.1, though. :)

### Post by mips on 2007-04-22
> **fistfullofroses said:**

In those days you kinda had to be 1337 to use a computer. It struck me more as a hobbyist past time. Many people programmed, mostly in Basic but many of us also coded Assembly the way 'real men' did :)

25 years of Spectrum - [_BBC news_]("http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/technology/6572711.stm") and the [_Original Commercial_]("http://www.newlaunches.com/archives/zx_spectrum_is_25_years_old.php")

### Post by forrestcupp on 2007-04-23
> **mips said:**
> In those days you kinda had to be 1337 to use a computer. It struck me more as a hobbyist past time. Many people programmed, mostly in Basic but many of us also coded Assembly the way 'real men' did :)

Many people also did home brew electronics projects for their computers. Some people actually built their own computers, my uncle did a 6809 based system from Elektor Magazine that was suppose to run FlexOS

Yeah, I did some assembly on my C64.  I knew what almost every memory location in the kernel ROM was used for.  I started to learn some C and Pascal, but they were pretty worthless on a Commodore.

I remember once running to Radio Shack and Munier's Electronics to scrounge up the parts I needed to build an audio sampling expansion card/microphone for my C64.  I was going to use a cassette tape case to house the electronics.  That was an amazing thing back then.  It wasn't a regular thing to get speech/audio samples on a computer at that time.  When I was about halfway finished with the project, I saw in a magazine that I could just buy one for $15.  So I scrapped my project and bought one.

---

### Post by ComplexNumber on 2007-04-24
i thought that this thread would be an appropriate place to put [this]("http://www.theregister.com/2007/04/23/spectrum_zx_25/") article. its about thezx spectrum being 25 years old today. happy brithday, zx spectrum ;).

> Clive Sinclair's ZX Spectrum is a quarter of a century old today. The machine that *really* launched the UK IT industry hit the streets of a depressed Britain on 23 April, 1982.

The Falklands War was properly kicking off, skirt-bothering Europhiles Bucks Fizz were number one with their unforgettable hit My Camera Never Lies [no, us either], and new romantic coal miners were using yellowed ration books as makeshift lavatory paper while they waited in million-strong lines for their Giro - if they weren't being beaten up by skinheads.


  Dark days, then. But lo, along came bespectacled Messiah Sir Clive Sinclair with the successor to his 1981 release, the black-and-white ZX-81 [see what he did there?]. The ZX Spectrum boasted a visual cortex-melting eight colours at 256 x 192 resolution, blistering 3.5MHz CPU, and crucially, a crisp-repelling vulcanised rubber keyboard.
  It gave a nation of spods a good reason not to go outside, paving the way for a generation UK IT triumphs, like the National Programme for IT.

All of us spend more time on computers than TV.  I was heavy into Feisty Alpha and Beta (with bruises); all the rest are on XP.

### Post by Znupi on 2007-05-23
From 1994 to 2006 I used this PC:
486 at 100Mhz (on Turbo, without Turbo: 66Mhz)
100Mb Hard Drive.
RAM -- can't remember, probably a few kb or maybe a meg.
Vdeo Card - absolutely no idea.

Which... in time... I upgraded to a staggering:
AMD K6/2 266Mhz
96Mb RAM
20GB Hard Drive
Ati Rage Pro 3D - 4Mb Video Card

Yup, this PC was running Win XP and... it felt like hell working on it. For one, I couldn't use Firefox, it locked the whole system up. Everything was soooooooo painfully slooooooooow..... And imagine, it's the PC on which I first learned how to code (I'm the whiz kid at school at informatics) -- I had a webserver installed on it, with PHP, MySQL, and all that stuff :D. I'm actually amazed it worked...

But now... now I have a mega monster! At least compared to my old PC:
Intel Pentium DualCore 2.80 Ghz :D
512Mb of RAM (it's the only thing that pisses me, it's too little!!)
160Gb of Hard Drive
An amazing Asus X550 Video card with 512 Megs of memory!!

It's a really nice, warm and fuzzy feeling when a window pops up in the same second you double click "My Computer".

### Post by IgnorantGuru on 2007-05-24
Commodore Vic-20 - connected to the TV set in the living room.  3.5K RAM.  To this day I still write some mighty tight code having learned on that machine - bloatware goes against every nerve in my body.  Stored my programs on cassette tapes - reading/writing a 1 or 2K file to tape took minutes!  I remember sitting there waiting for the tape to load (during which time nothing else could be done).  Back then if I wanted a program I had to write it! (or type it in line-by-line from a magazine, then try to debug my typos).  I also had a screaming 300 baud modem (I could type faster than it could transmit).  I could also get on the phone and whistle to its carrier tone and it would connect to me and start displaying random text.

Then a few years later I upgraded to an Apple //e with 64K RAM, monochrome monitor and a **DISK DRIVE!!!!**  (5 1/4 floppy that stored an amazing 180K I think)

Then an IBM model at some point, and I got this neat program called Multi-DOS that let you run several programs at once! I was in the butter zone then!

### Post by IgnorantGuru on 2007-05-24
> **king minger said:**
> Wow, I'd forgotten about that trick!
had one of those punches specifically made for the job with a guide to line it up in the right place :)

Oh so you were one of THOSE people - I remember seeing those in a magazine.  I did it the hardcore way, sometimes missing and having to take another chomp...  the disk would look like something took a bite out of it.

(For those who don't know what we're talking about, the 5 1/4 floppies had a write-allow notch cut in the side, which you could tape over to make it read-only.  But it you cut (or hole-punched) a notch in the other side, you could turn the disk over and write on the back as well as the front, doubling the capacity!)

### Post by jperez on 2007-05-24
The first computer I can rememer (vaguely) was an old Epson with ONE 5.25" drive with JUST  a prompt screen and one tank type game.  VERY heavy, very old and I can't even remember what the model was.

My first memorable PC was the Packard Bell Legend 10CD Multimedia Computer.  Specs:

CPU: Intel 486SX2 50Mhz
RAM: 4MB (Integrated - No Sticks)
HDD: 800MB (Might have been less)
OS: MS Windows 3.11 Windows for Workgroups (I still have the restore CD with the install files LOL)
CD-ROM: 4x Unknown Brand
Audio: Aztech Audio 16bit (I still have that card too)

Came preinstalled with:

Windows 3.11 WFW Basic Essential Software & Games
Voyetra Orchestrator
**Ski-Free** :>
Packard Bell Backup Image Software (w/ Funky Music while it created IMG files)

Yeah.  I had that system for about 4-5 years, if not more, before the power supply gave out on me.  Then we upgraded to a newer Packard Bell w/ Win95 :P

Still, I was the first one that most techs down in South Texas to be the first person ( I was 11 at the time) to install Windows 95 on my Packard Ball Legend 10CD and bypass the 8MB RAM requirement, installing it with only 4MB RAM using an old DOS memory extender.

Anyway, there you go! :D

### Post by yabbadabbadont on 2007-05-28
> **SonicSteve said:**
> 8mhz, or 12mhz if you pressed the turbo button. What was that thing for anyway? Who wanted to make slow even slower?

Those turbo switches, when not enabled, usually slowed the machine down to 4.77MHz as that was the speed of the original IBM PC.  (I'm not claiming that your's did though)  The switch was there for those who had games, or other applications, that used timing loops and that assumed that the clock speed was 4.77MHz.  I used to have a game called (I think) Popcorn (kind of like Breakout if I remember correctly) that was unplayably  fast with the tubo switch enabled.  My old Packard Bell 486 even has a turbo switch, although I haven't run any testing software to see how much it slows down the processor when not enabled.  Since I have DosBox on my current machine, I don't even use the old clunker any more.  :D

---

### Post by ticopelp on 2007-05-28
The first computer I ever had was a Commodore VIC 20. 3583 bytes of memory. Enough said.

I purchased my first PC from a local dealer... I think it was a Pentium 100. I never owned a 386 or 486. I was also the first person in my group of peers to have Windows 95 (hooray for me). 

What's really amusing is that the guys who put it together never bothered to boot it to see if it worked, which it didn't. After a lot of head-scratching and trying to make it boot, I and a friend finally found the culprit: a single line of text that said MAKE SURE TO CHANGE THIS LINE BEFORE SHIPPING TO THE CUSTOMER. The people I paid $2,000 to for the computer hadn't bothered to change it. I fixed it, and it booted fine. 

That's the first, last, and only time I've ever bought a machine off the shelf. I custom-build all my machines these days, because, apparently, I'm the only person I can trust.

---

### Post by old_geekster on 2007-05-29

Oh, yes, it had 128K of memory, which was allot compared to the 64K used by other desktops of the day.128 kibibytes of memory

### Post by gmc on 2007-05-30
> **matthew said:**
> It had some great games.


 ;-) You're bringing a tear to these ol' eyes.

Oh the good ol' days of hacking on the Coco.

Modding it for 64K Ram or soldering an inverter chip to the video chip so the display would be black background with green text rather than the other way around. Hell we even connected the Carrier Detect line on the serial port to one of the pins on the joystick port so that our home written (in basic) BBS could detect when someone hung up with out logging off properly...

Sigh! Those were the days.

Gord

Edited: Just looked up that list of games.. I'm surprised Color Baseball (one of my fav's) isn't listed. As I recall you could put tape over pin 32 of just about any cartridge and then save the game/program to cassette. I guess all those cartridge's are in a land fill some place now :-(

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2007-06-08
My first was a TRS-80 Model 1 running Level II BASIC. I got it used and had a lot of "fun" trying to find stuff for it. I eventually found a cable to connect a cassette deck, and a book which enabled me to type in my first BASIC program called "Shoot The M's".

After that computer, I moved up to a Commodore Vic 20. I began learning a lot of BASIC programming on that one. Then, I moved up to a Commodore 64. Now THAT was a fun computer! I also ended up getting a 8088 XT which I barely used and was infected with the Stoned virus. The C-64 just seemed so much more advanced - 16 colors and I could listen to SIDs (as opposed to computer speaker beeps). I also had a floppy that played a clip of the Holiday Rap on the C-64 along with some Porn and a strip poker game. I had two floppy drives and a printer for it. I REALLY wanted a modem so I could get onto BBSes, but my parents wouldn't get me one. :( And yes, I had a hole-punch accessory ;)

I also owned a Coleco Adam which was kindof a neat computer. The tape drive was fast compared to the C-64 one, and took special tapes. It also played Coleco games. The bad thing about that computer is you had to take the "data pack" out of the tape drive when you shut it off, or it would erase the tape.

Never liked the Timex-Sinclair. The keyboard was crap, 1k of ram (unless you had the 16k ram pack) black & white. I gave it away.

I also had a Commodore CBM for a short time. That was a hefty machine! The dual-floppy drive I had for it was about the size of two PC towers!

Then I moved over to a PC. No need to go into detail there. I still do some "classic computing" but in smaller form. I own a TRS-80 PC-3 Pocket computer, and a Sharp PC-1261 pocket computer. I don't have the room to keep things like the big CBM, but these can be tucked away nicely on a shelf. I also had to transfer all my C-64 floppies onto the PC using a special cable I made. I had forgotten the disks at my parents house and they (barely) survived a house fire. About 50% of the floppies made it (they were all water damaged and growing mould when I got them) but most of the stuff I programmed was saved. It's nice that I can use an emulator to run all the programs I wrote.

---

### Post by wofritz on 2007-06-08
My first computer:
Homemade Z-80 system, started with 16k RAM and TTY reader/puncher. No ROM, so bootloader must be entered with Hex-Keyboard (I knew it by heart). Programs were stored on punched tapes. Later upgraded to cassette recorder storage, then to 8" floppys and CP/M 3 OS (and a boot ROM!)

Last stage: 512 kB RAM, accelerated graphics (256kB graphics memory, 100000 vectors/s), 2 5.25" floppy drives.

It's still existing!

Wolfgang
[Yes, I'm OLD]

### Post by iceportal on 2007-07-20
I also owned an Apple IIe. My first computers were (simultaneously) the Apple IIe and Apple IIc.

I learned to write software on the IIe using a program called "Step By Step." It taught me BASIC.

Back in the day, they had 3-2-1 Contact magazine, which included a section called BASIC TRAINING. I was 9 when I first started programming.

Eventually, we got a Compaq Persario to replace the Apple IIe. I was disappointed, but later thrilled, as I learned C, C++, Java, QBASIC, and everything else... Needless to say my parents were worried.

I remember being so thrilled when, gasp, I found a REAL COLOR MONITOR!!! in my garage. My parents thought it was a broken TV, but I put it on the Apple IIe and it was... beautiful. Brings a tear to my eye.

Today's computers just don't quite have that sense of magic and wonder that they gave me back when I was a kid.

I was born in '86. Started using the computer around 1990 when I was 4, started programming in '95 when I was 9.

Oh... There was this one time my uncle let me borrow his Apple Macintosh (back with the black & white screens and old-school mouse). I saw a diagram in the user manual that showed the internals of the machine, so I decided I should take it apart to see for myself. I started poking screwdrivers in all over the place and ended up frying the whole thing. It never worked again. And I was hooked on computers for life.

---

### Post by dondad on 2007-07-21
The first computer that I actually used was a PDP8 that we loaded with punched paper tape. The first sorta PC that I used was an IMSAI that a group of us built. ### Post by sparks0548 on 2007-07-21

### Post by lisati on 2007-07-21
#1 Casio PB-100, little more than a programmable calculator that used a version of Basic, and once expanded had a whopping 1kb ram - eventually died after being trodden on

#2 A "VZ-300", a Z80 based machine, marketed in some countries with the name "Laser" or something like that. Eventually died circa 1992.

#3 A Wang machine, can't remember the model, a 286 machine running MS-DOS 3.30 at all of 10MHz, with a Z80-based "Wang office" network card that I never got round to using, and a 1.2MB 5.25" disk drive, monochrome (brown actually) Hercules-based display. 

#4 a "bitser" with some parts from IBM which on a good day reached somewhere around 20Mhz. Also used MS-DOS 3.30. 

#5 Digital Venturis 5133 running Win 98SE, 1Gb hard drive (later upgraded to all of 3Gb), recently resurrected, internal modem replaced with a USB card and hooked up to my home wireless network using a combined usb hub, wireless adapter, and other gizmos.

#6 (Desktop) and #7 (Laptop) both new enough to come with XP Home, and still going strong.

EDIT: Forgot 2 Commodore 128's sitting upstairs currently unused in the spare room.

### Post by amadeus266 on 2007-07-21
My first computer was the first version of the C=64 with a tape drive. The one with the buggy video chip. It also had a problem with the timing chips as they would burn out about every 8-10 months. It sure cost a lot of money to replace a chip that you can buy today for less than $5. When the C=128 came out I upgraded but couldn't find any software for it in my area so I still used it in 64 mode. I had one program written by Microsoft and I believe it was a terminal type program for connecting to a BBS system. I used it once and remember thinking "Why did the write something so clunky?" Running GEOS with a 1581 floppy, a 512K expansion card, 300 baud modem and a humongous 20MB HDD, I was able to do everything my friends were doing when WIN95 came out. Of course it finally gave up right after Win98 came out so I had to finally break down and buy one of those crappy PC's and that awkward Microsoft windows thing. I felt it was sluggish compared to my Commodore. I still miss my C=. Thank goodness for emulators. I have a DVD in my desk drawer with 8 different emulators and over 3000 games. For those times when I feel nostalgic. Oh, and about 15 hours of SID music!!!

### Post by Darkhack on 2007-09-06
I was just a little kid when I got my first computer.  It was a Packard Bell with 4 MB of RAM and it ran Windows 3.1.  It came with one of those noisy printers with the sheets all connected and dotted on the side and the print looked like a typewriter.  My second computer was an IBM Aptiva.  286 CPU and 16 MB of RAM, also running Windows 3.1.  Third computer was an HP Pavilion 6475z, 96 MB of RAM that was later upgraded to 192, 466 Mhz Celeron processor and 8 MB ATI video card, running Windows 98.  My fourth computer is the one I am using right now.  It's an Emachines T6212, but only because my mother wouldn't let me build my own.  She barely knows how to use a mouse and she thought I would break it if I tried to build it myself, even though I have built computers for many of my friends successfully.  It was running Windows XP SP2 and now has Ubuntu 7.04.  It has 512 MB of RAM with ATI xpress 200 integrated graphics.  Athlon64 3200+ 2.0Ghz.  This was back when 64 bit was a new thing to the PC maker market.  Up until then you had to build it yourself if you wanted a 64 bit CPU.  I was actually very surprised by the quality of the machine.  They have really improved since they were bought out by Gateway.  The case is very clean and organized.  This machine was definitely designed for upgrades.  Open slots including a PCI Express slot.  I like it a lot.  I don't know if I will build my next computer after this one or not.  It can be a hassle and is many times more expensive than just getting a Dell.

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-09-06
Technically, it belonged to my dad, but I was the one who used it: Commodore 64. The 64 refers to the number of kilobytes of RAM, for those who don't know. 

Back then we didn't *have *hard drives for home computers: we used 5-1/4" floppy disks, and they were actually *floppy*. And they held 350 KB. And we had to carry a whole stack of floppies to school, uphill both ways, in the rain *and *snow *and *sweltering heat, with no shoes, on a road paved with nails and rubbing alcohol. And we *liked *it.

### Post by nonewmsgs on 2007-09-08
> **IgnorantGuru said:**
> Oh so you were one of THOSE people - I remember seeing those in a magazine.  I did it the hardcore way, sometimes missing and having to take another chomp...  the disk would look like something took a bite out of it.

(For those who don't know what we're talking about, the 5 1/4 floppies had a write-allow notch cut in the side, which you could tape over to make it read-only.  But it you cut (or hole-punched) a notch in the other side, you could turn the disk over and write on the back as well as the front, doubling the capacity!)

oh yeah i was with you!  it would end up looking like 3 circles in there.  i loved my C64.  the one thing i didn't care for was that i typed faster than it could keep up with, so i probably don't type quite as fast as i could have by starting programming at such a young age.

### Post by Niall Stephen on 2007-09-11
OldGaf that is one neat PC :)

I feel positively ancient reading this thread ;)

The first home computer I owned was ... a Dragon 32. Built in Wales, it was loosely based around the Tandy TRS-80 Colour Computer and I got mine for Christmas 1982.  I'd been whining to my parents for a computer for an eternity .... trying to justify it on the grounds it would "help me with my homework" .... haha .... what a manipulative kid I was ....

Most of my mates had ZX Spectrums but my Father decided he didn't like the little rubber keyboard that the Spectrum sported so we ended up with the Dragon instead (my Father didn't really consider things like software compatability LOL).  

All I can remember doing on Christmas day, from about 8am til the family meal in late afternoon, is playing Star Trek on my parents portable TV  .... and of course doing my first programming in Basic which went along these lines .....

10  PRINT "NIALL"
20  GOTO 10 

Oh what great fun. The spec of the machine was as follows -

NAME  	Dragon 32
MANUFACTURER  	Dragon Data Ltd
TYPE  	Home Computer
ORIGIN  	United Kingdom
YEAR  	January 1982
BUILT IN LANGUAGE  	Microsoft Extended Basic
Basic Interpreter 1.0 (1982)
KEYBOARD  	QWERTY Mechanical keyboard
53 keys
CPU  	Motorola MC6809EP
SPEED  	0,9 Mhz
CO-PROCESSOR  	Motorola MC-6847 Video Display Generator
RAM  	32 kb
ROM  	2 x 8K or 16K EPROM comprising Microsoft Extended BASIC
TEXT MODES  	32 x 16
GRAPHIC MODES  	Several graphic modes, max : 256 x 192 (with 2 colors)
COLORS  	8
SOUND  	1 voice, 5 octaves with the Basic
4 voices, 7 octaves with machine code
SIZE / WEIGHT  	38 (W) x 32.5 (D) x 9.7 (H) cm / 2.1 kg
I/O PORTS  	TV connector, 2 analogue joystick ports, cassette port, Centronics parallel printer port, cartridge slot, composite monitor port
PRICE  	Dragon 32 : £200

 Software came from cassette tapes which seemed to take an eternity to load up. In UK there was a booming trade in weekly computer magazines which provided you with page after page of Basic programs, submitted by readers, which you had to carefully type in line by line to get games on the cheap. 

Dragon themselves had some success with the machine and went on to produce a 64kb model but where overwhelmed by the competition, mainly from Sinclair and Commodore, and went bust only a few years later.

My Father still has the Dragon 32 and it works even 25 years later. As does the Sinclair QL which replaced the Dragon and the Commodore Amiga which replaced the QL. He's adamant one day they'll be "worth good money". 

Hope springs eternal, huh ?

---

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2007-09-11
> Are there any good older computer (ZX Spectrum, C64) emulators for *nix i could fiddle about with?

You can install VICE. It emulates a number of Commodore computers. There's a tutorial on here (somewhere) on how to install it (do a search). I actually just installed it last Sunday and downloaded stuff from the Demo scene. That stuff was wild!

I actually picked up another REAL C-64 a few days ago. I'll be putting some stuff on floppy to run, just for old times' sake. I'll be writing about it in my blog in a few days (hopefully). 

I loved that machine. Too bad Commodore messed up their business so badly. We'd probably be running Amiga computers with Workbench on them instead of PCs with Windows.

BTW, anyone recognize my avatar? :)

Those who had nothing but PCs really did miss out on a golden age of computing. Computers were so much fun to play with back then. I don't know how many C-64s I fried because of Alienware's crappy Fastload cartridge. I finally got smart and chucked it.

### Post by magic-neophyte on 2007-09-15
The first computer I was allowed to play on was the BBC Micro. 

Brings back fond memories of 'poking sprites'.

Later I worked with MS-DOS 3. Always tweaking the AUTOEXEC.BAT and CONFIG.SYS to get games to work.

Ah, those were the days. Ubuntu allows me to relive them, with the BBC emulator and FreeDOS and DosBox. Wonderful, the magic of open source. Gives me some hope for humankind, in a darkening world. I sincerely hope Canonical will maintain their open and responsible style of Ubuntu sponsorship. Perhaps other corporations will realise that it is possible to be successful and have a highly ethical corporate structure as well, instead of going after megaprofits at all costs, using coercion, vote buying, etc.

PS: Such as Microsoft is doing at the moment in standards commissions in the EU,  to sabotage the acceptance of Open Office in governmental offices. Why do this, you ask. Well, a significant amount of European tax-income is spent on proprietary licenses of Microsoft software for government computers, handsomely lining their pockets out of public funds. Think of all those computers in courts, libraries, schools and universities. Don't get me wrong, I am not against Microsoft, but against their dirty ways of doing business, stifling competition by using maffia-practices to influence governments.

See ffii.org for more info.

### Post by FrankVdb on 2007-10-20
My first computer was a Commodore 128 I bought back in 1985.

Jesus Christ, where has the time gone... My cousin had a C64 but I wanted to do better so I bought the 128. In the end, I ended booting into C64 mode all the time because there was simply no software written specifically for the 128.

The machine also came with a sort-of DOS system, called CP/M. That was the third mode you could boot into when firing up the machine. 

I still have my old machine. I still have two boxes full of 5 1/4 inch floppies with all kinds of stuff on them, mainly games. 

Does anyone know whether they would still work?

### Post by jgrabham on 2007-10-22
> **FrankVdb said:**
> An IBM PC running BASIC? That must've been MS-DOS or Windows for Workgroups 3.11!

The first PCs did run BASIC

> 	

		
NAME  	PC XT - Model 5160
MANUFACTURER  	IBM
TYPE  	Professional Computer
ORIGIN  	U.S.A.
YEAR  	1983
END OF PRODUCTION  	Unknown
BUILT IN LANGUAGE  	Microsoft BASIC
KEYBOARD  	Full-stroke keyboard with numeric keypad and function keys
84 or 101 keys
CPU  	Intel 8088
SPEED  	4.77 MHz
CO-PROCESSOR  	Socket for a 8087 math co-processor
RAM  	From 64k to 640k, depending on models
ROM  	64 kb
TEXT MODES  	80 x 24 / 40 x 24
GRAPHIC MODES  	CGA modes : 320 x 200 / 640 x 200
COLORS  	16
SOUND  	Tone Generator
I/O PORTS  	eight internal slots (five 8 bit ISA), RS232c, Centronics
BUILT IN MEDIA  	One 5.25'' FDD, 360k (3.5'' on later models)
10Mb or 20Mb hard-disk
OS  	MS DOS
POWER SUPPLY  	PSU built-in
PERIPHERALS  	Numerous IBM and third-parties expansion cards, i.e. the QuadRam 512 KB RAM card
PRICE  	$8000 (Complete version with 640 KB RAM, 10 MB HDD, colour display)

### Post by Tux Aubrey on 2007-10-22
[IMG]http://cafelinux.org/forum/gallery/74_23_07_07_7_02_08.png[/IMG]

A 1984 Dulmont Magnum - one of the first commercial laptops.  It ran CP/M from a floppy and was powered by an  Intel 186.  ###

This was a machine I used for work (and even managed to send telexes with it over a modem).  It had a very basic WP and a maths program.  I also admit to using Windows 3.0 on an XT at home that year.  Commander Keen and Jill of the Jungle appeared soon after. 

Before all that I was using a Wang mini for running stats programs (on casette tapes!) at university.  Oh, and learning fortran on a mainframe with manually punched cards.

### Postscript confession - I left that job in about '86 and took the Magnum with me (it was completely outdated by that time and nobody there had a clue what to do with it anyway). A few years ago I donated it (dead) to the Sydney Powerhouse Museum collection of IT bits.

---

OS9 by microware is fairly similar to Unix, cli driven. very powerfull stuff.

### Post by Mr. Ksoft on 2007-10-24
My first computer was a Mac Performa 600, bought when I was roughly one year old.  Came with a 32mhz 68030 (which was really running much slower due to the way the P600 was made) 5MB RAM (upped to 20 from what I can tell) a 120MB hard drive (it's pretty much full now), and one of the first built-in CD drives (the caddy type ones.  Also, it doesn't play burned CDs at all, it's so old!)

I got it out of my house's attic a few months ago and got it started again (tried to get it running for over a week before I realized I had to replace the PRAM battery) and noticed that it was actually quite slow, even with all the upgrade work and a few tweaks I proceeded to apply to the old System 7.1 monster.

### Post by moshuptrail on 2007-10-26
How about a PDP-8e with 16k ram and an ASR-33 (115 baud) terminal and paper tape reader.  (That's really 115 baud, not 115k - that's about 10 characters per second)

I used a programming language called Focal, (precursor of Basic) and an assembly language called SABRE.  

Yes, you could really write some pretty sophisticated programs in 16k.

To boot this thing you had to literally toggle in about 20 16-bit words so it could read about 40 feet of paper tape.  I think it took about 1/2 hour to boot.  I don't remember any other storage media besides the paper tape.

---

### Post by jflaker on 2007-10-28
Vic-20 which got zapped by a lightning strike, I upgraded to the Commodore 128(which also had a "dual boot" to the Vic-20).

Had 128K or ram plus a 64K expansion cartridge, 2 modified floppies which on a switch could be reconfig'd to be dev 8 or 9...........DAT drive and several CPM programs.

It was a screamer at 4Mhz........I can't believe we are approaching 4Ghz.........

I still have it, but my floppies were erased by the heat and cold of 20 years in the attic!  The hardware is fully operational!

### Post by daengbo on 2007-10-29
Tandy Model I, circa 1978-9

Z80 processor, 4KB RAM, no drives whatsoever. BASIC was on the chip, so it booted almost instantly to a prompt. I had a tape player for it to save and load programs at 300 baud, but there really wasn't much available.

I remember typing in programs from the back of the magazine I got every month, then combing them for the mistakes I'd made.

### Post by ushills on 2007-11-02

### Post by lisc998 on 2007-11-02
A Commodore Pet with no case. I got the monitor, the circuit board and a tape player. I got it to work, but I had to build my on keyboard out of veroboard and microswitches. I had some inside help getting it going and getting the bits - it was all stuff scheduled to be thrown away. After that the zx81 seemed tame. 

By 1985 I was developing on a Burroughs Medium System (V Series) mainframe. It was massive, with 10 Mbytes of memory.

### Post by ruibernardo on 2007-11-02
My first computer was a [Timex 2048]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timex_Computer_2048"), a kind off spectrum 48K (Z80 processor). I've read the BASIC manual and made my first program there (a little game where you bought and sell shares with RANDOMIZE). Played on it a lot too.

The first PC I've put my hands on - as a student - on was a Amstrad PC1640 (8086 processor @ the stunning speed of 8 MHz, almost twice the 4.77 MHz of the standard 8086) with 640 KB of RAM and a 10 MB hardisk with MS-DOS 3.3 and Hercules display (B&W). It wasn't the worst one, later this one broke and for some time I was in a [Amstrad PC1512]("http://www.old-computers.com/museum/computer.asp?c=183") with 512 KB of RAM and no hardisk, but 2 diskette drives of 5 1/4 inch and EGA/CGA colour display and Windows 1.0 - my first GUI!

The first real owned computer I bought was a Intel 386SX (16 bit bus), 2 MB of RAM and a 40 MB hardisk, DOS 5 and windows 3.0.

And the story goes...

### Post by asmiller-ke6seh on 2007-11-21
My first computer ... a Xerox 820: O/S CP/M, 64K RAM, 8" floppy disk with 384K capacity (IBM 3780 floppy), 80 x 24 black and white display (no graphics capability), parallel port with a Xerox 620 daisy wheel printer at 20cps, serial port with a 300 baud model for connection to THE SOURCE. This computer was released 5 months before IBM announced their first PC.

I still have the original technical specification manual, with the Xerox Monk ("It's a Miracle) on the cover.

[IMG]http://terowe-ivil.tripod.com/sitebuildercontent/sitebuilderpictures/.pond/monk6.jpg.w180h165.jpg[/IMG]
The Xerox Monk, played by Jack Eagle, was the mascot for Xerox Corporation for about 10 years. He was also the original APPLE in the Fruit of the Loom ads back in the late 70's. Here he is at a Xerox company function back in 1987.

### Post by sajro on 2007-11-21
My first computer was a Commodore Amiga. I don't remember the stats but my dad got it secondhand in the early 90s for around $500. 

It didn't even have a hard drive (minus the one holding the OS) but stored everything on floppies. I had a Carmen Sandiego game I absolutely loved. And a battlechess game I wish I could find a version of for Linux (I learned chess from that). I might make one someday! 

OH! And some game called Zerg that was a graphical grid based adventure game. And a game called "Labyrinth" that was a very violent text game taking place in what I guess was a university. It must've been from the UK considering how it referred to things. It was (and is) my most absolute favorite game ever. I beat it once and managed to map out the entire thing. I'd get lost for hours in that. I miss that, surprisingly.

Until I remembered about this, I thought Airstrike was the best game ever. Now it's like the 5th.

I miss that computer now. Dang.

*snip*

EDIT: Got the E-UAE emulator working. I'm playing Zerg!

### Post by sajro on 2007-11-21
I can't seem to find a ROM (.adf) for the game I called "Labyrinth". It may be called something different as I always get results for a mixed graphical/text game based on the movie "Labyrinth".

In case you may remember its name (in which case please PM me) it said things like:

> 
You are at the crossroads.
> walk north
You are at a locker room.
> look
You see a key ring and a broken bottle. Brian Bitterman is here.
> take bottle
You take the bottle. Brian Bitterman attacks you.
> attack brian
You attack Brian Bitterman
Brian Bitterman attacks you.
> attack brian
You attack Brian Bitterman with a broken bottle. Brian Bitterman dies. He leaves: closet key, crowbar.
> take all
You pick up the key ring, the closet key, and the crowbar.


That's my best recollection. Commands and such are most likely inaccurate. Please help if you remember this.

### Post by sleepingdragon on 2007-11-28
Hell, now this thread brings back memories! 

Mine was an Acorn Electron 32K. Anyone in the UK remember the BBC Micro? The Electron was the baby brother to it...

Well... let me see into the past as I light up my smoking pipe and set the rhythm on my rocking chair...

There was no VGA output, I was plugged into a small portable colour television. The joystick was only available via a special adapter which cost £40/$80 at the time. I still remember the command "*JS ON" which allowed me to specify which keyboard controls the joystick was meant to emulate. Good thing was that the joystick emulater worked 100% on all my games. The adaptor also allowed 6 other ROM cartridges to be inserted for applications.

Programming was done via BASIC, which turned out to be a half-decent version. No compiler of course, commands were interpreted on-the-fly, but even at 1Mhz, it was fairly slick, and like a lot of the first home computers with their OS on ROM chips, it loaded up a lot faster than PCs today! I noticed in the news recently that Asus were offering motherboards with an in-built OS... certainly nothing new there! They're just reinventing what was around only 20-25 years ago.

Games then had something a lot of new titles don't have.... gameplay! Graphics aren't important. Enjoyment of the game is. I've had more fun Nethack than some graphic-intensive titles! I remember such classic titles as "Sphinx Adventure" and "Thrust". The ability of the Electron to use a cassette deck was limited by its sensitivity to the volume control on the deck. Probably something to do with signal/noise ratio.  

Talking of graphics, 16 colours was the order of the day with resolutions that were lower than most mobile phones!

Oh, some happy memories still lay with the Electron, and I wouldn't have swapped it for anything else. Now, gerroff my laaannd!

---

### Post by dysolve on 2007-12-09
My first computer back in the early 90's was a dicksmith electronics thing, the person who bought it never knew hpw to use it so she was going to throw it out. SO she gave it to me , it had a tape drive and a keyboard and you pluged it in to the TV. all the tapes she gave me where blank, so I went out and bought a book and just copied straight from the book to command line. I made a game where you flew a plane over building that kept getting taller, and i made a word processing program with spell check.. now that was a god send.. I remember handing in assignment to school on the fan fold paper which had one blue line then one white line and so on LOL.. 

My first real computer was a 286sx? 4meg ram (8 sticks) 1 or 2 meg video and 100mb harddrive, had only DOS but i go myself a subscription to a BBS with FIDO and download games.. If i remember right it was early 90's and it cost AU$1300 it weighted 50 plus KG and the video card was bigger then my current mobo.. 

my best computer to today was my pentium PRO 90MHZ .. with 64mb ram and 550mb drive and cd-rom.. with 16mb video..

### Post by jeffbilling on 2008-01-30
Wasn't really mine but it was what I worked on for nearly 2 years every day - usually 10-12 hours a day.
A PDP-8
4K
No screen
No keyboard
All coding done manually and then entered in location by location through the magic 12 keys on the front of the computer.
We had a paper tape that loaded the basic program but had no way of producing a new paper tape when we changed anything in the program.
So you loaded the paper tape, keyed in all the changes (tape was nearly a year old so lots of changes) keyed in your new code and prayed.
Input was paper-tape 
Output was paper-tape.
The job the software did  was to create hyphenated and justified tape from raw data to drive hot-metal printing machines
I think it cost the companies about £20 000 and I earned about £1000 p.a.
Not bad for a 4K machine!!
P.S. I still have a copy of my first major job - hyphenating Finnish text to an accuracy of 99%.
Still the best thing I ever did!!!
Happy days.
:guitar:

### Post by Kevbert on 2008-01-30
My first computer was a Sharp MZ80K with 34k of RAM, built in cassette deck and   mono monitor.  I upgraded it to 48k with a soldering iron.  The Basic interpreter came on a cassette and had to be installed each time I wanted to run it.  I got a bit bored with this so I added an Eprom programmer and Eprom memory board so that the computer would boot-up in a few seconds.  It's a shame that you can't do that for Windoze or Linux.

---

### Post by kernelCompile on 2008-01-30
My first comuter was a used PC with an AMD 386DX 40MhZ processor, 120mb hard drive, DOS 6.22 and Windows 3.1. I put an early version of Slackware Linux on it (from a CD that came with a book on Linux). This was maybe 1994 or 1995. Back then, the kernel (1.2 or 1.4 I think) was monolithic, and the stock kernel for the distribution had drivers for every piece of hardware built in, so the book advised that the first thing you should do was compile a custom kernel, with only the drivers you needed. The Slackware distro had a few decent games (full screen VGA graphics, mostly), but otherwise wasn't too useful (Then again, neither was Windows 3.1. It would lock up every time you sneezed.)

---

### Post by PET3! on 2008-03-07
Aaa.. :)

The memories just flood in.

My first puter was a Spectravideo SVI-738. ...it was an advanced
piece of equipment, back in 1986 when my father bought it for
me and my brother. :)

the machine was an MSX-1, that had a more advanced graphics chip, and a built-in floppy disk. :P

all those Konami cartridge games. ...and the cassette games back then.
Copying them was a sort of science. ;)

The first PC in my family came 2 years later in 1988. ...it was a Hyundai 8088- IBM clone, with an amazing 640kb of RAM, an advanced EGA Graphics card and display.
...and a HUGE 32MB HDD. :P ...the floppies were 5inch, and had 360kb of pure storage space. :P :D The OS was MS-DOS 3.21, and it had Windows 2.01, if I recall correctly.
and the games, jeez..those were indeed the days. ...prince of persia, leisure suit larry, paratrooper, grand prix circuit, police quest, etc. :)

Great thread, brought it all back again. :)

### Post by Therion on 2008-03-27
My first computer?  That would have been a Radio Shack [TRS-80 Color Computer II](http://www.thesvd.com/SVD/Images/coco2big.gif) (aka the Coco II).  ASCII graphics, portable TV for a monitor, cassette tape drive.  Went all out and upgraded that bad boy to the full on 64MB of RAM though.  As I recall the 5.25" floppy disk drives were $100 so I had to mow quite a few additional lawns for that (anyone else remember using a paper-punch to double-side their floppies?)...  Oh, and a 300 baud modem. 1200bps modems were out, but were outrageously expensive at the time.

Gawd... How many hours did I kill typing in programs gotten from [Rainbow magazine](http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/The_Rainbow_-_June_1984.jpg/250px-The_Rainbow_-_June_1984.jpg)?  Tandy BASIC (and hacking ROM packs) for the win!!!

### Post by pbpersson on 2008-04-12
My very first computer was a Commodore VIC-20

I bought it for $80 at K-Mart around 1983

It came with 4K of RAM - I spent $100 to add another 16K memory module.

In the early days I had to re-type each program every time I wanted to run something because I had no way to save anything.  Then I bought a cassette drive for the thing and that was SUPER high tech  :)

A friend came over and I was showing off the computer - back then having a computer in your house was really something.  I typed in this large program, we debugged it and got it working - seemed to take forever.  Then rather than saving the program to cassette I decided to print it.  I got up to get something to drink, tripped over the power cord for the computer, and that was the end of that program.  :(

Ah.....the good old days......I can laugh about it now :lolflag:

### Post by stoodleysnow on 2008-04-12
My Auntie Christine gave us her old Amstrad CPC 464 in about 1995. It had about 80 game cassette tapes. A few years later my Mum's cousin Jeff gave us their prized CPC 464 with another 40 games because some vandal had broken the monitor. So for a while we had 2 464s, 120 or so game tapes (some with several titles), 5 joysticks of which at least 3 worked and a CTM 644 Monitor. 0_0
Our first 'PC' was an old Elonex 386 box of about 33MHz and 8MB RAM. (I think). About Year 2000 I think. It ran Windows 3.1 slowly and with some difficulty. We had a load of floppy disks with games for it. That and a couple of 'Wordpad' documents was the limit of its use. In 2001 we upgraded to a Compaq Deskpro with Windows 95, Intel Pentium MMX 233MHz CPU and an amazing 32MB of RAM. Nice though it was, I positively hated being stuck with 16 colours. Those graphics drivers were seriously poor for the S3 Trio and Virge cards.
In 2002 my Uncle Peter gave us his old Smile computer. Same spec except 64MB of RAM and it had sound. I was then given the old computer (Compaq) and used it for a while.
In 2004 I bought a Packard Bell Multimedia machine of about equal spec except for having 92MB of RAM, for £8.00 at a car boot sale. I then dismantled the Compaq and broke it. On reassembly it died. I blame welded CMOS batteries.:lolflag:
I ran a pirate copy of Windows XP on the Packard Bell and we even had Dial-up internet. The whole experience was fairly slow to start with, and pretty soon I had overfilled the 2GB of hard disk space. Music, school work and small hard drives don't mix.
Then things changed. I started asking folk for old computers they wanted to get rid of, which they gladly gave me. I teached myself how to dismantle and rebuild them. I upgraded to a newer Packard Bell with a 1GHz PIII CPU and 20GB hard disk, and 512MB of RAM, which ran XP rock solid (surprisingly, perhaps). The MSI motherboard had built in sound which was fine, and despite it getting capacitor plague, my soldering iron and a capacitor from a donor board saved the day (and the up-to-then intermittent USB ports).
I started building computers ready to sell to people, but the ICT technician at school pointed out that I couldn't just sell pirate copies of Windows XP, and following a little curiosity regarding what ran on the school server he showed me Linux. Skeptical at first, I quickly realised that Linux is far better than Windows. The rest is history :-)

---

### Post by dsiembab on 2008-04-17
first computer, that's a good one. it's not really the computer but how much you spent for the computer that really interest me. When I walked into circuit city about the summer of 97, when a 3gb hard drive was all you really needed. I must of had the glow of sucker, but hey I wanted the top of the line computer without having the top of the line knowledge about computers. so I bought a Compaq Presario 4772, everything was preinstalled and the thing that sold it was the video camera, back then it was a big deal I guess. I still can't believe I slapped down about 1600 dollars for it, when I could've put that money into a cd or mutual fund or better yet google stock and waited ten years. it cam with a top of the line pentium mmx processor, I really can't remember all the specs but I thought it was pretty cool. I still have the pod game that came with it. I actually used that computer till about 3 years ago, so I got my monies worth considering the tutorial on how to take apart a computer and put it back together again which came on the install cd for compaq's at the time. can't find the golf 95 slash magic carpet cd though it's probably around here somewhere. well this is besides the trs-80's and commodore-64's.

### Post by Robertjm on 2008-04-27
Sinclair!! Don't remember the model number, but I think they only had one. You used to create programs by enterring basic onto the screen and then recording the program onto audio cassettes!! If the power went out you'd have to run the audio tape again to put the program back into memory.  

Fun for a youngster (you didn't say first USABLE computer :)  )

First usable computer was something my Dad bought off a friend. Don't remember what it was other than it was pre-IDE. The first usable computer I bought was a Tandy 1400LT dual floppy laptop that was on close-out from Radio Shack. While it didn't have a HDD, it was pretty useful for the junior college student. I forget which DOS it was (2.0 or 3 I "think") and you'd load the OS into memory by putting Disk 1 of 4 into the driver before hitting the power switch and then when prompted insert the others. Someone actually posted an article on hacking the machine to install a HDD. You had special order a EPROM from Radio Shack to allow the HDD, which I did. Its STILL pinned to my cork board unopened.

### Post by GrandpaLeaman on 2008-04-27
In 1978, I bought my 1st computer. It was a Tandy TRS-80 (TRS=Tandy Radio Shack). It had a whooping 4 KB RAM and cassette tape storage. I wanted to upgrade to 16 KB RAM because when writing programs, I kept running out of memory. The problem was I would have to ship it off for the upgrade, and the cost at that time was prohibitive.

I used the TRS-80 to learn BASIC language programming, which I have since forgotten. I remember writing random number generators for the die rolls in AD&D, as well as random monster generators. So there I was, the DM, with my stack of books, my maps, and my TRS-80 computer (which was not all that small). It was a good thing that the friend who's house we played at had a large dining room table.

Anyway, I really would not want to go back to THAT computer!

---

### Post by SirThom on 2008-04-27
Apple //e.  It had the 80 column card, also a 1200 bps modem and a daisywheel printer.  I envied my friends for being able to print out pictures and graphics, and they envied me for my text looking to crisp and sharp.

I got a book from the library on programming in BASIC that happened to be written for the Apple //e, and taught myself.  Later my dad set me up with a ProDOS disk so that I could save files.

At a Radio Shack I saw a PC, quit out of the sesame street colouring programme to try to write programmes on it, and found that I couldn't just execute basic statements from the command line.  The employee said he was going to set it up so I could use it, and saw that he had reloaded the paining programme.  I didn't really know how to respond to I clicked a couple spots to be polite and wandered off.

---

### Post by SirThom on 2008-04-27

I knew that the brand was connected with both Tandy and Radio Shack, but I had decided that it was just a cluster of letters that they thought would make a good name.  Sort of like how car companies use meaningless letter and number designations (and sometimes made up words) to name their cars.

### Post by HyperHacker on 2008-06-11
The first two don't really count because they weren't functional, but I'll list them anyway. :P

#1: A Fisher-Price computer toy thing. You'd slide these cards into it, which had holes for LEDs to shine through and pictures on them, and it asked a question and you pushed the button corresponding to the correct answer. Obviously that was a long ***-time ago, so I don't really remember how it worked.

#2: A Commodore 64. I was told it didn't work, and I certainly never did get it to do anything useful (we had instructions to load one game, but it ended up with broken graphics that made it unplayable), but maybe I just didn't know how to work it. >_>

#3: A 66mhz 486DX, with 16MB of RAM, 88MB of hard disk space, 256 colours, and Windows 3.1. We kept it until the hard drive died. (Windows started asking me to insert a disk in drive C. O_o) I learned a lot about computers and hacking just by fixing the problems that cropped up on it. :P Like when I extracted a zip file, and the file inside had a space in its name; the silly thing went right ahead and created an inaccessible file, and I had to fix it by opening the hard disk itself in a hex editor and renaming the file. That was fun. Also the first time I did any hardware work, replacing the floppy drive, which was a good bit easier than I expected, although I managed to unplug the hard drive and plug it in backward, and almost panicked when I saw the mess of garbage on the screen that resulted when I turned it on again.

### Post by ellis rowell on 2008-06-13
The first computer I used was a mainframe at British Telecom, the second one I used was in 1986, the Sinclair QL (often referred to as the Quantum Leap or Queer Lump). But it gave a good basic training in computers, especially when it stopped working and you had to open the case and prise up the processor then push it down to get a better contact in the socket. In the days before gold plating of the contacts we often got corrosion on the contacts.

Many modifications were done to the motherboard, I was highly amused to see an engineer get his soldering iron tangled in the jumpers he was soldering in. We had to take the plug off and draw the lead out.

Ah! Happy Days.

### Post by shivans on 2008-07-08
My first PC experience came in the early 80's when my parents bought me an **Oric 1**.

Here are the specs for it:-

Manufacturer Tangerine 
Processor 6502A 
Speed (MHz) 1 
Ram (KB) 48 
Ram expandable to (KB) 63 
ROM (KB) 16 
Sound 3 channels with noise 
Number of keys 57 

Interfaces 
8-bit parallel port
34-pin expansion port
cassette interface (7 pin DIN)
RGB socket (5 pin DIN)
 
Did you have anything to rival that 1mhz powerhouse?

### Post by Traumadog on 2008-07-15
My first computer.......  

Radio Shack TRS-80

It possessed 4K of RAM...
Had a standard cassette tape recorder for long term data storage...
Utilized a single font and produced only capital letters...
Came with a monochrome monitor...
And had a BASIC interpreter that only allowed for 2 string variables...  A$ and B$.

When I got it I couldn't believe that such powerful technology could be purchased for home use.  :)


By the way......  I still have the thing!!! :)

### Post by mb-editor on 2008-07-15
Used my first PC in 1986; these were called 'PC-XT's and had 5 MHz chips with a 'turbo' button that overclocked it to 10 MHz - didn't seem to make any diff, though!  It didn't have a hard drive, booting was through a floppy (DOS 3.1 I think), then we went on to use Q-basic, basic-A and stuff.

Later on, I went up the ladder to Lotus 1-2-3, WordStar (versions 2.0 through 7) and dBase III+ before graduating to Windows 3.1.  Then there were the games - Digger and Prince of Persia were our favourites!

I bought my own PC much much later - they were simply too expensive - and still use the same one 6 years on, though there's nothing original except the cabinet and the 17" CRT (planning to put in a 22" Wide TFT soon...)

Aah, nostalgia...

### Post by mollyj on 2008-07-27
My first computer was an Acorn one (pre-dated the electron by a couple of years) 256 bytes of ram and 512 of Rom; hex keyboard and everything was in machine code; didn't even have an assembler. My wife had it mounted in a picture frame for our 25th wedding anniversary years ago. Probably doesn't count as a 'real computer'; In that case my first 'real' one was the UK101 a self build 8k 6502 based machine from the pages of wireless world or practical electronics (can't remember which). Had a rudimentary basic but if you wanted graphics you had to use assembler and block characters. 
In those days it was more about skill with a soldering iron than the gui.:)

### Post by Ichido on 2009-07-29
In 1987, when I was 35yrs., I purchased my first "PC" which was a
Commodore 64 (64kb RAM), Phone MODEM, One External 1541-II 5 1/4" Floppy Drive (Used "Single Sided" & "Double-Sided" Disks), 14" CRT Monitor, Mouse, Dot Matrix Printer (Black Ink Tape).
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodore_64](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodore_64)

Desktop/O.S. was the GEOS, "Graphical Environment Operating System",Running Commodore BASIC Ver 2.0.
It was not difficult to write your own Programs!
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GEOS_(8-bit_operating_system](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GEOS_(8-bit_operating_system))

The "Internet" was BBS (Bulletin Board System), the Fore-Runner of the "Post your Message and read other Posted Messages" which is similar to "Facebook Walls".
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board_system](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board_system)

Favorite Games:
#1) Hacker: The player takes the part of a hacker, a person experienced in breaking into "secure" computer systems who accidentally acquires access to a non-public system. If they can hack in, they can gain control of nifty gadgets and access a secret global network. The game was shipped with minimal instructions to get the game going, but no information on how to play, thus building the concept that the player did hack into a system.
While exploring the "System", the System would from time to time "Discover" your Break-in and ask you an obscure question (from your previous Hack-in), which if you didn't know, the System (Game) would then Shut Down! One needed to keep many notes during your Hack-ins.
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hacker_(video_game](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hacker_(video_game))

#2) A Nuclear Reactor Simulator. Cannot remember Title, but will update when I find the Software.

#3) A Flight Simulator, in "Wire-Frame" Graphics. Explore Hostile Planet, find Minerals and Objects while battling Alien Space Ships.

I still have my old C-64, which was still working when I Upgraded to an IBM-PC, running Windows 3.1 on PC-DOS 5.

---

### Post by Phossy on 2009-07-29
A Superboard II in the late 70's - it ran Microsoft Basic and was probably the cheapest computer to do so at the time ( I remember also looking at the Nascom 1 and Tangerine). It had a whopping 4 K of RAM and i used it to programme games for my own use. At the time computer mags like Computing Today would print programmes (often over months for the really big ones, that you could type out. How things have changed.

The Superboard was followed by an Oric, then a C64 and an Atari ST before entering into the world of PC's. I still have the Superboard and Oric. The Superboard and Atari were my favourites.

### Post by crotig on 2009-08-23
Ahh, the nostalgia.

My dad brought home a ZX81, with tape recorder and 16K memory pack, when they were first released thinking it would help him with his accounts. He quickly got sick of trying to use Vcalc, or was it Vucalc, and I used it to play a pacman type game till my sister broke it.

I wanted an Atari 800XL for my 7th birthday but my dad bought me a Mattel Aquarius, [**Spec here**]("http://oldcomputers.net/aquarius.html"). This was made even more unamusing by the fact that was the in the UK, nobody had ever heard of this computer and there were virtually no games available for it.:(

2 Years later I got the Atari 800XL I wanted with tape drive. I remember well the agony of waiting 45 mins for a game to load an my sister coming in and knocking the cable before it was finished so I would have to start again. She was EVIL!!. I got a 5.25 floppy drive later on and used to swap games through the post.

I then got an Atari 520STFM, built in disk drive, Oooooh!.I recall well it's version of a windows desktop. I was fanatical about the game Carrier Command, never completed it.

I did not have a computer from about 1995 to 2003 when I got my first PC. Athlon XP2000, 256MB, 40GB HD, Onboard gaphics(quickly upgraded to Geforce card),DVD-ROM, CD-Writer, Windows XP.

Upgraded to Athlon XP2600+, 756MB, 80GB HD,40GB HD , Geforce 5700, Dual layer DVD+-RW, Still XP.

Now Use Athlon 4800X2, 2GB, 320GB HD, GForce 8800, XP and Windows 7.

Also have Acer Aspire5310 laptop with XP, But will be going to Ubuntu soon

And last but not least my little Eeepc 900 with XP, may also be going to Ubuntu soon.

How times have changed.:lolflag:

### Post by Mike Rettig on 2009-08-23
Ohio Scientific C4P. 8k of ram running a Coosol Data Logger printer (made from a kit). I had to put a switch on the front panel of the console because the baud rate on the printer and the tape drive didn't quite match up. Adjusted the baud rate with a pot and switched it in and out with the switch. I used an RCA TV that I converted to a monitor using information in Don Lancasters' TV Typewriter Cookbook.

### Post by deadbeatdrum on 2009-08-23
Not so much my first computer, that was an Acorn Electron (the same UK company that produced the early BBC computers).

One the of the  earliest computers that I saw in the flesh, and wish I had now,  was the Sinclair ZX80. I have seen many ZX81's and early 16k and 48k Spectrums, but only one ZX80.

Maybe an original mint condition IBM of the same era (packaging, manuals, guarantee card etc) comes close.

### Post by The Real Dave on 2009-09-09
It was a HP Pavilion, from maybe 04? Probably around a 2GHz PIV, 256 or 512Mb of RAM, and a 60Gb HDD. Unfortunately, that computer filled with dust and fried itself before I knew anything about computers really. We donated it to a geek guy we knew, he was only too happy for the parts. We replaced it with an Acer Aspire T650, which I'm happy to say is now faster than ever running 9.04, the only upgrades being 3 new fans (80mm at the front and back, 120mm on the side), another Gb of RAM to bring it up to 1.4, and a 320Gb SATAII HDD (Mobo is SATA though). I wanted to upgrade to a Pentium Dual Core, but unfortunately, after a bit of digging, and advice from some people here, I found that the Mobo is a custom Acer one, designed for my chip and Graphics CHipset. So no dual core for me :(

### Post by chessnerd on 2009-09-09
A Gateway 2000 desktop in 96... I recently found it in the garage. It was SO dusty on the inside, and the dust had clumped into dark gray gunk from the years of humid summers and cold winters. It was pretty bad, but it cleaned up alright.

As for the OS, well, it's in pretty sad shape. The desktop icons are not functional. Programs everywhere are corrupted. But the desktop still has a picture of me on it (clearly I was the favorite).

It was a pretty reliable little machine in its hay day. We had it for 6 years before it died on us and it ran Windows 95 the whole time without a single reinstall.

The specs were great for their day: 200Mhz Pentium I, 32MB of RAM, and a 2GB hard drive. It also had a sound card, video card (I'm guessing 2MB), and internal modem. It cost my parents well over $1000 and that was even with a deal my dad got though his office.

Now it sits in the garage again and I don't know what to do with it. Windows is trashed and I can't find the disk for it. It certainly can't run Ubuntu, or Xubuntu, or even Fluxubuntu. DSL is probably the only modern distro that can run on it. Also, it doesn't have a free PCI slot to put in a network card and without Internet it seems kinda pointless to put a new OS on it. It does have a few ISA ports left though, and if I can find a 10MB ISA network card it might yet rise from the grave...

---

### Post by moody_mark on 2009-09-15

### Post by mike67114 on 2009-09-18
Honeywell 6000 mainframe in the basement of the School of Business at the University of Kansas in 1974. ((This machine replaced the one that was destroyed by a bomb set by anti-war terrorists during the Vietnam war. The IBM chain printer on that machine continued to output while laying on its side in the rubble outside the building -- until someone cut off the power and lost all the jobs in the que.))

Procedure was to find an unoccupied keypunch machine, punch-up your program on computer cards, take the deck to the dispatch area in the computer center, beg the clerk to take your undergrad submission, wait at least 24 hours, then come back and retrieve your 14-inch wide print out from one of the cabinets. _IF_ you remembered the JCL cards and your program compiled you would get results--or not--depending on your programming skill.  

later, as a second year student- I gained a password and authority to use one of the thousand or so remote terminals on campus. The terminals were connected by modems and real telephones. You dialed the phone number of the remote server, waited for the tones and hash, then jammed the phone into the rubber mounts on the modem. You got 30 minutes of access, then had to redial.

Ain't technology great!!

---
