### Post by jonallport on 2009-09-18
OK, here we go:

The FIRST computer I had was a Dragon 32 - Meteoroids on a ROM cart, superb!

Dragon died, got Acorn Electron - First taste of programming (BBC BASIC)

I think my brother also acquired a Sinclair ZX81 from a car-boot sale aroung this time, but it never really got used

Many years later...
Dad brought home a used PC from work - 8088, 640kB RAM, 20MB 5.25in HDD, 5.25in FDD, Hercules graphics - kept using this up to about '98, well after I'd got newer kit.

Then I bought myself a Psion series 3 PDA - still wish I had this, but part-exed for a PalmPilot (fool)

First PC I bought myself was a 486 DX2 66 (Yeah! Feel the slipstream!!!) came with 400MB HDD and OS/2 from Escom (remember them?).  Administrative bungle meant I 'accidentally' got a 500MB HDD in it after it went away for (i think) re-imaging.  4MB RAM upgrade for this machine cost me GBP117.50 and I never got the native OS/2 drivers for the (VESA local bus) graphics card.  This machine was stolen when I was at university and was replaced with...

Pentium 150, 16MB RAM (+ upgraded secondary cache to aid RAM upgrades at later date), wait for it...
...1.7GB HDD, running Win95 OSR2

Also, during the reign of the P150, i bought a second-hand Toshiba Satellite notebook - P75, 24MB RAM, ~750MB HDD - I was the envy of all at work while they worked on their Wyse terminals attached to SCO UNIX I could run Quake at a blistering VGA resolution with amazing un-surround, tinny, mono-sound!

A clear out at work yielded a DEC Microvax 200 (A VAXStation 2000 without the on-board graphics header) which got spirited away to the spare room at home.  Never really got to grips with the VAX, it went into a skip when I patched Girlfriend Beta1 to Fiance 1.0 with the 'Diamond' feature pack and then found that Wife 1.0 was incompatible with VMS.

This is the point where it get a bit too modern to be interesting, but I will say that the P150 is still alive and well-ish with 48MB RAM (See, I told you the upgraded secondary cache would be worth it) and is in the care of my mother-in-law who finds it difficult to grasp the concept that this machine can store not just one, but MANY letters and lists on its cavernous (and down-graded) 800MB HDD.

---

### Post by TiredBird on 2009-10-12
Polymorphic - don't remember the model;  
little metal orange box; 
8080 processor; 
16KB ram (on 4 - 4KB boards); 
OS and Basic on a 1K byte ROM; 
storage on an audio cassette player;
TV for output; 
had a real keyboard though. 
Cost: not sure, probably between $500 and $1000
(It came as a kit. I put it together on my kitchen table.)

The first factory assembled personal computer had not yet happened. 

It did though eventually and I still have it:  Apple II serial number 11. 
48KB of Ram, now "upgraded" to 64KB.
Cost: $1595.00
TV for monitor;
storage on a cassette player;
OS in ROM
Basic in Rom (written by Bill Gates);
Mimeographed instructions.
That was somewhere around 1975 I think.

### Post by kfitzenreiter on 2009-10-12
My first computer was the one, the only Commodore 64k.
 
I had a BLAST with that computer.  I was just reading an article about some Ubernerds that managed to build some sort of network interface to fit into the card slot on the back of that beast and they got it on the internet.  It almost brought a tear to my eye....
 
In high school I remember using Apple IIe's.  Then in college it was IBM PC's with the dual 5 1/4 in floppy drives or if you were lucky with one 3 1/2 in floppy and maybe, just maybe a tiny 20 MEGABYTE hard drive.
 
I had the BRAIN virus.  It ruined one of my assignments.  Thank goodness I made a backup on a second floppy.
 
I love old computers though.  Sometimes it's fun to load up DOS and windows 3.11 on an old pc and try to get it on my network.  It's a lot of work and if all else fails you can still sit and play Reversi or Minesweeper or whatever.
 
I've often thought, wouldn't it be cool when intel or AMD gets up to 64 cores to bring back the commodore name only it would be the Commodore 64 CORE with, I don't know, something like 64 Gigs of Ram.  The classic light blue screen could be a bios starup splash screen.  Oh, well, just a dream...

### Post by Old_Grey_Wolf on 2009-11-08
> **ronrut said:**
> Radio Shack color computer (CoCo).  8086 processor, cassette tape until a single-sided single density floppy drive became available.  This was around 1982.  I remember upgrades in memory cost a fortune.

Ron

My CoCo had the 6809, 8-bit CPU, with a clock speed of 900KHz. I upgraded it to 32KB of RAM from its original 16KB. I also upgraded it with the 78KB floppy drive when they became available. The floppy was much more convenient than the audio cassette tape drive.

That computer cost me about 10% of my yearly salary. I bought it to learn more about programming. I wrote some games for my children, accounting programs, word processors, etc. I did more programming on that computer than I have done on computers I have owned since then.

I bought a Lenovo recently for less than 0.5% of my yearly salary.

The KHz and KB in the post are **not** typos.

---

I've used computers since 486 era, my dad had a comodore 64 among other things but not in working order. The first one that really got mileage on it was a compaq Cyrix which was a bit inferior to a pentium ( as in the original pentium ). I remember just listening to mp3s would overwhelm my system so I started using dos based MP3 players, then someone told me about this OS that could use IRC and MP3 on a command line simultaneously and was free to boot.

Too bad Linux circa 96 had horrendous hardware support for dial up modems.

---

### Post by ChadMMc on 2009-11-08
wow, I feel old!

 The first computer I USED was a hewlet-packard 2114B mini-computer. (6' high, 3' wide' 4' deep, a whole 10kb of RAM, a modified army teletype for a console (no monitor), and a marked card reader for program storage and loading) (this was back in '75, when I was in high school).

The first computer I actually owned was an Atari 520-ST which was considered to be almost top of the line back then (I think around '85) ... had the GEM OS which was neat. (the only other systems that I know of that used it were pc's put out by Xerox corporation (Ventura Publisher anyone?).

):P

### Post by ezsit on 2009-11-08
It was a Tandy TRS-80 Model III with 32Kb of RAM and 32Kb of ROM and an external cassette machine for storage. I remember writing BASIC games and running the CP/M operating system. 

Loading programs from cassette was a real test of patience, starting the tape machine and going to get dinner before some programs would be loaded in memory, ready to run. Wow!

Then it was on to the Apple IIE and a floppy disk drive, what an upgrade! I remember writing BASIC programs that utilized an attached VCR to deliver interactive, multimedia training programs for college students at Brooklyn College, and I was only 13 years old.

---

### Post by sonny123 on 2009-11-08
I had a dual processor MAC in 1980!!!!!!  
Now before you start with.... "but Apple Mac wasnt born until 1984" 
This was a an Entex electronics MAC. I was 10 yrs old and it was the coolest Xmas present i ever got.
Just check out the spec.

16 LED display matrix
8-digit fluorescent numeric display
7 octave range
2 micro processors (TMS 1600 N2LL) (TMS 1170 NLHL)
TMS 1024 NLL Input/Output Expander
6,000 Bytes of ROM
1,024 Bits of RAM

I spent the entire holidays programming a cricket game loosely based on cricket dice and programing Human League songs.  
My Dad was so impressed by the fact i could programe it he ordered a VIC20 for my birthday in February 1981 but then convinced me to wait for the TI99/4a release. and  I got a brand new TI 99/4a delivered from th USA in the summer of 81 and was the envy of most of the lads in school. 
My mum & Dad had a huge row about how much it cost, I think it was about £500 in 1981, but my dad justified the cost because i wrote him a program which balanced his company cheque books and broke out the VAT and he reckons the savings in accountant fees paid for both machines within a year!
[IMG]http://t1.gstatic.com/images?q=tbn:ZLJ9Dk6L3J6KqM:http://technabob.com/blog/wp-content/uploads/2009/08/first_mac_computer.jpg[/IMG]My First MAC [IMG]http://t1.gstatic.com/images?q=tbn:mIO5Dwhs2iryyM:http://www.99er.net/markwills/ti994a.jpg[/IMG]the TI99/4a

### Post by jflaker on 2010-02-11
First was a Vic-20, which succumbed to a thunderstorm and I was upgraded by the retailer to a Commodore-128 as Vic-20 was out of production for over a year....I had a 128K RAM expansion cartridge, a tape drive, 2 floppy drives both with toggle switches glued to the outer case to switch device numbers.....and a 300 Baud modem which I later upgraded to 1200 Baud.

I still have it + all peripherals and floppies in my parent's attic.  It all still worked last time I powered them on about 5 years ago....

### Post by bakechad on 2010-02-13
> **dondiego2 said:**
> Like a lot of people it looks like, my first was also a Vic-20.  After that I bought a C-64 with my paper route money and I thought I was the top geek in the neighborhood at the time because I was accessing bulletin boards.   That was in the early 70's.

I think you mean the early 80's.  The Vic-20 came out in 1980.  My story is exactly the same, started with the Vic-20 and saved up to upgrade to the C-64.

The BBS scene was really a unique experience looking back.  Do you remember the relay email networks, where you would drop an email on the "network" and the BBS would sync with the master server and you might get a response in 2-3 days.  Now we get upset if email isn't instantaneous!

### Post by n0njy on 2010-02-16
My FIRST computer? Was a slide rule actually.  An old wooden one my dad gave me for HS electronics classes.

:)

My SECOND computer was one I built.  I used an 8-bit 6800 Motorola processor, had 4K of random access memory, and it was programmed in machine code using push button switches and LEDs.  Later, I added an outboard to it that had a BASIC interpreter ROM and a keyboard so I could enter basic code.  Oh I could save programs on a cassette tape too!

I took a small 9" TV and converted it to a monitor (no, I didn't use a modulator!) and I used to play Star Trek on it (remember that old game? with 64 sectors, klingons, stars and the occasional black hole, Romulan and some other things I don't remember).

My SECOND computer was an APPLE ][+ and my first real "game" was Temple of Apshai!

(Do any of you remember any of those things?)

---

### Post by gnomeuser on 2010-02-16
The first machine I remember having in the house had a green on green monitor. I think it was an AT but I honestly don't remember much about it outside of the fact that it had a really nice tic-tac-toe game.

The first machine I remember using was the second i386 machine to ever arrive in my country. 16 screaming MHz and 2 MB of ram, 20MB of harddrive.

The first machine I owned and paid for myself was a used Amiga 500. After the Amiga died I bought a 75MHz Pentium and have stayed in PC land ever since.

### Post by Lord Stig on 2010-02-16
1995 onwards...
IBM XT 4.77MHz 640KB RAM, 10MB HDD
IBM 286 (for 4 hours - till the 20MB hard drive died!)
Atari STFM 8MHz 512K RAM, no hard drive
Ambra 486SX 25MHz, 4MB RAM->12MB RAM, 240MB HDD, 4x CD-ROM, sound card
IBM/Cyrix P133MHz overclocked to 166MHz, 48MB RAM, 1.7 and 4.3GB HDD, CD-RW & DVD-ROM
Celeron 667MHz, 64MB RAM, ? HDD
Athlon 1GHz, 640MB RAM, 40GB HDD, DVD-RW
Acer laptop, Celeron 500MHz, 48MB RAM, 8GB HDD (I think)
Pentium4 2.6GHz, 1.3GB, 160GB HDD
Toshiba M100 Portege laptop, 1.2GHz Pentium M, 768MB RAM, 160GB HDD
Pentium D 2x2.8GHz, 1.3GB RAM, 80GB HDD

All fond friends. (Some) gone but not forgotten.

### Post by xouns on 2010-02-16

Last year I bought a new one, my first computer on which a spent money.

### Post by Desert Sailor on 2010-02-16
My first computer was an IBM 5150 dual floppy (8-inch) with 64K of memory.  Green monitor and I think I paid close to $3,000.00 for it.  Ran DOS 1.1

I remember when I upgraded one of the floppies to a Seagate 10Meg full-height hard drive, the sales guy told me I would NEVER fill it up.  Cost about $1,000.00 

The next machine for me was a PC-XT/turbo from PC's Unlimited (what is now Dell computer) had a little switch on the back which would shift it up to about 6.33 Mhz I think...not sure I just ran it in turbo mode all the time.  Amber Screen and I was stylin.  By then I was using DOS 3.1 (best DOS ever).  I used Word perfect from "Satellite Software" and Lotus 1-2-3. 

About that time I started building my own machines from parts and cards and haven't had anything but "home-brew" since then.  

Fun walk down memory lane...Thanks guys.

### Post by markbuntu on 2010-02-18
I had a 286 once. It might have been an XT. We sold some of them with dedicated ship plotting software running windows2.1 on top of DOS3 with 128k ram and monster 10MB hard drives in the early 80s. It was stable and tiny and fast. Windows 3.0 killed all that. The poor app devs spent the next 15 years fighting windows buggy API and eventually lost out to soem guys from USSR who ran their app in a virtual linux machine.

### Post by MSich on 2010-02-19
I guess the first computer I had was a VIC 20, but it's really hard to call it a computer.  We really only used it for games, and it wasn't better than the Atari, so we didn't get a lot of use out of it.
 
The first computer I personally bought was a Tandy 1000 HX.  I got it in 86 or 87 while I was in college.  It had an 8088 processor, 640k RAM and a 3/12" floppy (fairly high-tech at the time).  No HD, that was a little pricey for me.  It did come with DOS 2.11 loaded in ROM and an integrated software package called Deskmate.  It had a rudimentary word processor and spreadsheet app.  I did many college papers on that machine.

---

### Post by ratcheer on 2010-02-19
My first computer was the tiny **Timex Sinclair z80**. I bought it at K-Mart. When I took it home and tried to use it, it rebooted itself every few minutes. I finally noticed a pattern - every time it rebooted, I was hearing a car drive up the street. I went to my window and tried it a few more times. Sure enough, every time a car passed by, the computer reset itself, I suppose because of RFI from the cars' ignitions. And my house was about 75 feet from the street!

I returned the computer the next day. I figured they would think I was crazy, but they gave me a refund just because they wanted satisfied customers. So, I coughed up a few extra bucks and bought a **Commodore 64** from them. I then used the 64 for several years.

Tim

---

### Post by matthew on 2010-02-19

That is so similar to my experience. Mine started with 4K of RAM, which I eventually increased to 32K. I remember the day I discovered that a hole punch could be used to effectively double the capacity of a floppy disk. Oh, yeah, I was in geek heaven. :)

---

### Post by Old_Grey_Wolf on 2010-02-19
> **matthew said:**
> That is so similar to my experience. Mine started with 4K of RAM, which I eventually increased to 32K. I remember the day I discovered that a hole punch could be used to effectively double the capacity of a floppy disk. Oh, yeah, I was in geek heaven. :)

Hahaha...something similar here as well.

When the warranty ran out on mine, I opened the case. I discovered that all I had to do was move a jumper to double the RAM. It already had the larger RAM chips in it.

I didn't know about the "hole punch" trick. :) 
160KB floppies would have been amazing.

:lolflag:

Then a decade later, modular programming was the next "innovation" spewed by the gurus. DUH, what in the heck did we do when we only had 4KB of RAM...brake the code into modules? :D:D:D

### Post by samalex on 2010-02-20
I don't remember if I ever replied to this thread, but here's a rundown of the systems I had, starting in 1986 when I was 11 yo.

1986 - Tandy TRS-80 Color Computer 2
1989 - Tandy TRS-80 Color Computer 3 
- Got my first modem and started BBSing this year as well - @300bps!
1990 - 8088 XL Clone running MS-DOS 3.3 (moved up the DOS versions but retired it running MS-DOS 6.2) 
- First computer I used to touch the Internet around 1993 through a local BBS
1994 - Packard Bell Legend 486DX-50Mhz system with MS-DOS 6.2 and Windows 3.11 
- My first computer to touch the WWW using college SLIP connection and MOSAIC
1997 - Home built 486-75Mhz (I think) dual booting MS-DOS 6.22/Windows 3.11 and Red Hat 5 
1998 - Sony VAIO Desktop Pentium-300Mhz system Dual Booting Windows 98 and Red Hat 5 then 6
2000 - Dell Dimension Desktop running Windows 2000 Server as primary Desktop
- Dropped Windows for Red Hat Linux 7 in 2001 after getting a virus! -- Linux full time at this point!
2000 - Dell Inspiron 3800 paying Microsoft Tax for Win 98, but loaded Red Hat 7 after it arrived
2001 - Home Built box running dual P3-1Ghz processors.  
- Used for desktop at first with Red Hat, then SuSE, then Slackware.  Now it's a home server running Ubuntu 8.10 Server.
2002 - Apple PowerMac G4 - Dual 867MHz (mirrored doors) - OSX Panther (I think) 
- Bought system more to check out OSX but moved to it full time in 2003 when my Dell Dimension desktop had a HD crash
- Retired in 2007 and started using iBook as primary system
2005 - Retired Dell Inspiron replacing with Apple iBook
2009 - Retired Apple iBook replacing with System76 PanP5 running Ubuntu 9.04 desktop

The dates and versions are from memory, so some may be off a small degree.  Plus I had a few other systems cross my desk through the years, like an Atari 800XL, Commodore Amiga 500, Apple II, Mac Classic, etc, but most were just hobby computers and never used full time.

For now our home server is still that Dual P3-1Ghz system and my System76 PanP5 is my primary system for everything.  My wife still uses a MacBook and my iBook is in the kitchen for streaming audio, recipe look-ups, weather, etc.  

Take care.

Sam

### Post by MoebusNet on 2010-02-20
I wish I could remember the first computer I ever attempted to use. My Dad belonged to the local Computer Club; he was always picking up obsolete computers that had been donated to the Club. He would buy them at a Club auction, but never managed to get all of the peripherals (sigh). At any rate the first one was some type of computer with a membrane keyboard that ran BASIC and used our TV for the display. I found out that a cassette recorder could save input for later use, but since school required hard copy and we didn't have a printer, I quickly lost interest.

The first computer I ever owned that actually did anything useful was a used Apple Macintosh 512ke and dot-matrix printer; you could actually do some word processing and print it out! I gave that one to my daughter to use for school, because I discovered that no-one within a hundred miles knew anything about Apples or sold anything for them.

The first computer that I could get onto the Internet was a custom-build 133 Mhz Pentium with (if I remember correctly) 128 MB RAM, 1 GB hard drive and a 28.8 kb modem running DOS 3.0 upgraded to Windows 95. Together with a HP5P laser printer and a SCSI scanner, I think that system set me back $8500! I would have bought Apple, but in December of 1995 I read that Apple had declared its intention to concentrate on the business market and abandon personal computing. That may have been FUD, but it worked on me.

I discovered Ubuntu somehow while "surfing the Net" (we actually talked like that) and after trying several very early distros managed to get up and running with Dapper Drake. It was maybe a year before I quit Windows altogether!

### Post by Darth Penguin on 2011-04-04
**Texas Instruments TI-99/4A**

> The Texas Instruments TI-99/4A was an early home computer, released in June 1981, originally at a price of USD $525. It was an enhanced version of the less-successful—and quite rare—TI-99/4 model, which was released in late 1979 at a price of $1,150. The TI-99/4A added an additional graphics mode, "lowercase" characters consisting of small capitals, and a full travel keyboard. Its predecessor, the TI-99/4, featured a calculator-style chiclet keyboard and a character set that lacked lowercase text.
[CENTER][img]http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/1979_TI-99-4_with_Speech_Synthesizer%2C_RF_modulator%2C_keyboard_overlays.jpg/300px-1979_TI-99-4_with_Speech_Synthesizer%2C_RF_modulator%2C_keyboard_overlays.jpg[/img][/CENTER]

Available peripherals included a 5¼" floppy disk drive and controller, an RS-232 card comprising two serial ports and one parallel port, a P-code card for Pascal support, a thermal printer, an acoustic coupler, a tape drive using standard audio cassettes as media, and a 32 KB memory expansion card. The TI-99/4 was sold with both the computer and a monitor (which was a modified 13" Zenith Color TV) because Texas Instruments couldn't get their RF Adapter FCC approved.
In the early 1980s, TI was known as a pioneer in speech synthesis, and a highly popular plug-in speech synthesizer module was available for the TI-99/4 and 4A. Speech synthesizers were offered free with the purchase of a number of cartridges and were used by many TI-written video games (notable titles offered with speech during this promotion were Alpiner and Parsec). The synthesizer used a variant of linear predictive coding and had a small in-built vocabulary. The original intent was to release small cartridges that plugged directly into the synthesizer unit, which would increase the device's built in vocabulary. However, the success of software text-to-speech in the Terminal Emulator II cartridge cancelled that plan. (Most speech synthesizers were still shipped with the intriguing door that opened on the top, although very few had the connector inside. There are no known speech modules in existence for those few units with the connector.) In many games (mostly those produced by TI), the speech synthesizer had relatively realistic voices. As an example, Alpiner's speech included male and female voices and could be quite sarcastic when the player made a bad move.

---

### Post by ubuntu1sttimer on 2011-04-05
It was about 1977 and my 1st computer was a hand built one from Radio and Electronics Magazine. A small business in Champaign, IL designed it and sold bare motherboards that you built up. The processor was a 2650 eight bit unit. Ram was 2k, roughly 2000 8 bit bites. All programing was hand assembled It used a TV for a monitor. 16 lines of 32 characters. I built a wooden box to house it. As to what it could do, it gave me my first knowledge of what a computer was and how they worked.

---

### Post by jhonan on 2011-05-05
Commodore 64 was the first one I owned (or should I saw pwned?)

Before that, the first one I *used *was a terminal with a printer attached but no screen - I don't even know what kind of mainframe it was connected to - But I remember playing Lunar Lander with the output going to the tractor-feed dot-matrix.

Around about the same time (in school, I was about 11yo) I wrote my first code in BASIC, to calculate the volume of a cylinder. I filled in the squares on punched cards using a pencil, and they sent the deck off to a lab somewhere to run the program, then posted the printed output back the following day.

### Post by majorpay on 2011-05-05
My first PC was a commodore 64.  

"Poke 53281, 0" and load "*",8,1  ftw!

My dad brought various computers home and they were downright ridiculous.  We had a Zylog and I think it was a TRS-80 or 120, and a few DECs.  One of the PCs was this gigantic monstrosity built into a desk with a screen and some comically over-sized disk drive.  

He also you used to bring home defective hard drive platters from the lab that were larger than your average frisbee for us kids to play with.

I'm not *that* old, it's just times have changed a lot in the past few decades.

### Post by 23dornot23d on 2011-05-05
TI 99 ..... with high resolution graphics ..... what a joke that was ...... blocks as big as your fingers ..... saw my cousin had a .......
ZX spectrum with sprites wow .... so went and bought one of them ..... 147 pounds I think  they cost back then .......
Ti 99 .... cost 400 pounds .... and could not do half the things the spectrum could ..... without paying for expensive addons ........ :confused:

I remember getting a separate maths co-processor for my first 486 machine and carefully putting it in place ..... as it was a lot of money and had tiny pins lots of them to line up and gently shove into place almost cockled to one side ...... ahhhh ..... those were the days lols ....
:D

### Post by madmax75 on 2011-05-05
IBM Aptiva (pizza box model), bought in 1996, ditched in 1999

3,5" diskette drive
CD-ROM drive (read-only)
100 MHz processor
16 MB RAM
500 MB hard disk
28.800 kbit/s modem
14" CRT display
Windows 95

Ah, the memories... :) I remember that Win95 came with some nice videos to showcase it's "multimedia" capabilities ("multimedia" was the buzzword then). I really enjoyed the Weezer music video (the "Buddy Holly" song) and the "Rob Roy" trailer :-D

Oh, and I DID have a genuine, honest-to-god Commodore 64 as a kid! With the horrid Datassette tape drive, complete with games on C cassettes. I just wasn't very good in using it. I don't remember what happened to it, though :(

My friend had the Amiga 500. Boy, was I jealous.
